When executed with proper methodology, it provides the most direct and actionable competitive intelligence available to sales, product, and marketing teams — but the majority of programs fail to generate lasting impact because they get the fundamentals wrong.\n\nThe difference between a win-loss program that transforms competitive performance and one that produces ignored reports comes down to five methodological choices: who you interview, when you interview them, how you structure the conversation, how you analyze patterns, and how you operationalize findings. Each choice compounds on the others.\n\n## Sample Size and Selection [...] User Intuition\nUser Intuition\n\n# Win-Loss Analysis Best Practices for B2B: The Complete Framework\n\nBy Kevin, Founder & CEO\n\nEffective B2B win-loss programs share five characteristics: balanced sampling across wins, losses, and no-decisions; interviews conducted by neutral parties within 14 days of the decision; question designs that explore the full buyer journey rather than just the final outcome; analysis frameworks that distinguish systemic patterns from one-off events; and operational feedback loops that translate findings into specific changes across sales, product, and marketing. [...] Avoid the common trap of treating every finding as equally important. Prioritize patterns that appear in high-value deal losses, that recur across multiple quarters, and that are addressable within your current resource constraints. A competitive gap that appears in 60% of enterprise losses is more strategically important than one that appears in 20% of mid-market losses, even if the raw interview count is similar.\n\n## Operationalizing Findings Across Teams\n\nThe most common failure mode for win-loss programs is the last mile: translating insights into changed behavior across the organization.\n\nTitle: How to conduct Win-Loss analysis in B2B markets - Adience\nURL: https://www.adience.com/blog/how-to/how-to-conduct-a-win-loss-analysis/\nContent: 1. Be careful when selecting potential interviewees, and be open to interviewing multiple individuals within an organization  \n 7. Determine Which Deals to Analyze\n\nYou don’t need to interview every buyer. Aim for a representative sample across different segments, sales reps, and deal sizes. Choose the Right Interview Method\n\nWhile surveys and sales notes can provide basic insight, live interviews (via phone or Zoom) are the gold standard for win-loss analysis. These allow you to ask follow-up questions, clarify responses, and detect nuance in tone and language.\n\nIf internal resources are limited—or if you want objective feedback—consider partnering with a third-party firm to conduct interviews. Buyers may be more candid when speaking to a neutral party. [...] You can also share these insights across product, sales, and customer success to help the entire organization stay in tune with how the market is evolving. When treated as a continuous feedback loop, win-loss analysis becomes more than just a postmortem—it’s a living input to your overall product marketing strategy and your ability to win in-market.\n\n## Final Thoughts\n\nIn a crowded B2B SaaS landscape, the companies that win aren’t always the ones with the best product—they’re the ones who listen closest to their buyers and adapt accordingly. A well-run win-loss analysis program gives product marketing a powerful lens into what matters most during the buying process.\n\nTitle: Win/Loss Analysis: What Is It? How Do Programs ... - Growth Velocity\nURL: https://growthvelocity.com/win-loss-analysis-definition/\nContent: logo\nlogo\n\n# What Is A Win/Loss Analysis\n\n# What Is A Win/Loss Analysis\n\nWin/Loss Analysis is a bottom-up, inductive methodology for identifying and understanding the issues that cause deals to be won or lost.\n\nData about individual sales opportunities is sourced either from sellers or directly from buyers. An analyst then uses purpose-built software to form theories about the key causes of win/loss outcomes.\n\nWin/Loss Analysis provides a novel and objective assessment of the issues and opportunities in a business’s go-to-market strategy.\n\n## Why B2B Technology Leaders Implement Win/Loss Analysis\n\nBusinesses in technology markets underperform when their leaders rely more on experience and market knowledge than they do on data. [...] ## Upleveled Win/Loss Analysis Is A Competitive Advantage\n\nB2B technology markets are complex and change rapidly. Fewer and fewer are relying on experience and market knowledge alone.\n\nGartner reports seeing this shift in its most recent Gartner Engine for Analytics & Research (GEAR) survey. Inquiries from B2B technology companies about Win/Loss Analysis increased by 5X.\n\nPrograms for Win/Loss Analysis often begin with an in-house analysis of CRM data. [...] Win/Loss Analysis is well suited to these rapidly changing conditions because it takes a bottom-up, empirical approach. But those static tools don’t give you the precise insights you need to make a difference.\n\nThat’s why win-loss analysis is perhaps the most powerful (and underutilized) source of competitive intelligence.\n\nWin-loss analysis exposes not only what your competitors are doing, but how they’re positioning against you, where they’re finding success, and most importantly—where they’re falling short.\n\nSurface-level competitive intelligence is like reading tea leaves—it might make you feel informed, but you’re missing the real story. [...] ## What Your Competitors Don’t Want You to Know\n\nTraditional competitive intelligence focuses on what competitors want you to see: their features, pricing, and public positioning. It’s a comfortable illusion that keeps sales teams busy with battlecards and feature comparisons.\n\nBut our analysis of over 6,000 B2B deals reveals an uncomfortable truth: your competitors aren’t winning where you think they are.\n\nThe win-loss feedback from these deals exposes three clear patterns—patterns that reveal what competitors hope you never discover:\n\nMarket leaders who understand these patterns use them to predict and counter competitor moves before they happen.\n\nInstead of reacting to individual deal outcomes, they spot competitive trends and neutralize them early.\n\n### Turning Patterns into Action [...] Why? Because when deals are lost, sellers default to the usual suspects: pricing was too high, features were missing, or that old classic—”the decision was made before we got there.”\n\nBut through win-loss analysis, buyers tell a dramatically different story. You can expose pricing strategies, sales tactics, and hidden vulnerabilities that your competitors don’t want you to see.\n\n## The Hidden Battlefield of B2B Sales\n\nWhile most companies play competitive whack-a-mole with outdated intelligence, their savvier competitors are operating on three distinct layers:\n\nMost companies never venture beyond the visible layer. After all, it’s comfortable there—everything can be neatly compared in spreadsheets. It serves both as a projection tool, helping your practice anticipate the success of a program, and a performance measure to determine whether the time, money, and resources invested are paying off. The calculation hinges on two factors: your practice’s upfront investment and the measurable return that follows.  ...    - Technology and systems, including EHR integration and reporting tools.  ...    - Stronger performance on value-based contracts, including quality bonuses and shared savings. - Lower use of acute and post-acute care for patients.\n- Reduced length of stay for hospitalized patients.  ...    - Direct reimbursements through CCM, APCM, and related billing codes.  ... Program costs include staffing, technology, outreach, and day-to-day operations. What can be harder to identify are the savings and revenue sources, since many of them don’t show up directly on a balance sheet. That figure is then divided by your program costs to show the ratio of what you gained—or lost—relative to your investment. They take the guesswork out of the equation and enable you to understand what is and isn’t…\n\nGoogle Docs\n\n## This post is for subscribers only\n\nSubscribe now\n\nAlready have an account? [...] 1. Prepare for the interview: Familiarize yourself with the customer's background and their interaction with your company. Customize questions based on their specific journey through the buying funnel.\n2. Conduct the interview: Use the questions provided in the template to guide your conversation. Ensure you cover all three stages of the buying funnel: awareness, consideration, and decision.\n    Awareness: Ask questions about the customer's initial recognition of their problem and how they discovered potential solutions.\n    Consideration: Inquire about how they compared different providers, the criteria they used, and their impressions of your solution versus competitors.\n\nTitle: Win-Loss Analysis Interview Questions: A Comprehensive Guide\nURL: https://www.sybill.ai/blogs/sales-win-loss-analysis-all-you-need-to-know\nContent: ### 1. Designate Ownership\n\nThe first step is assigning a specific person or team responsibility for win-loss analysis. This ensures the process isn’t neglected amidst competing priorities.\n\nWho Should Own It?: Ideally, win-loss analysis should involve collaboration between sales, marketing, and product teams. However, appointing a dedicated analyst or tasking the competitive intelligence team with ownership can streamline efforts.\n\nWhy It Matters: A clear owner ensures accountability and consistency, preventing the initiative from falling through the cracks.\n\n### 2. Build a Framework for Consistency\n\nTo avoid reinventing the wheel for every analysis, create a sales win-loss analysis template framework that can be used consistently to drive results.\n\nElements of the Framework: [...] ### 2. Respect Stakeholder Time\n\nYour interviewees, customers, prospects, or sales reps are busy. Being prepared shows you value their time and ensures the conversation is productive. Not indulging in questions and answers without meaningful insights can save you time and effort.\n\n### 3. Avoid Personal Bias\n\nWithout a structured approach, it’s easy to let personal biases creep into the analysis. A prepared interviewer focuses on facts, not assumptions.\n\n### 4. Drive Actionable Outcomes\n\nThe ultimate goal of sales win-loss analysis is to drive change. Preparation ensures you collect actionable insights to share with key organizational stakeholders. Preparing for the interview can help you determine the desired outcomes and insights in advance.\n\n‍ [...] ‍\n\n## Key Sales Win-Loss Analysis Interview Questions\n\nNow that we’ve set the stage, let’s dive into the meat of the matter: the questions. Some common challenges and quick fixes to overcome them include:\n\n### 1. Low Participation Rates\n\nTitle: [PDF] Win-loss Interview Checklist | Klue\nURL: https://klue.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/02/WinLoss_Interview_Checklist_Klue-1.pdf\nContent: Try to get a full win-loss interview scheduled with the buyer, but be willing to settle for a quicker hit—have a Typeform or template ready to fire off via email that they can complete on their own. Sure, in-person is better, but not everyone is going to be receptive, especially a buyer who went with a competitor. Mix short- and long-answer questions. You want to make them feel comfortable enough to share but keep it factual and actionable, at the same time. Use “Tell me more about that…” and clarify their points when you can i.e. “So, what I understand you saying is…” You can use a third-party to conduct win-loss interviews but it’s not necessary. Internally, CSRs are the logical picks because they’re removed from the actual deal, so no one feels awkward; yet, they have deep [...] WIN-LOSS INTERVIEW CHECKLIST Why is win-loss analysis important? Successful B2B growth requires continual assessment of process and per­ formance. If you’re not evolving, you’re stalling. Your best source of primary intel is the direct line you have to your prospects and your customers. That feedback is the foundation for strategic and tactical decisions, training and onboarding, product development and service. So, everything. And, if you’re not asking the right questions and making changes, you will repeat mistakes, waste time and resources and—ultimately–fail. Whether you’re selling widgets or wombats, your single most important mis­ sion is to understand your customer. Win-loss interviews are how you do that. [...] How was your sales support perceived? How could it be improved? What are the expectations for implementation? Are they being met? (They will change - keep asking this.) If anyone won, who was it? What was the deciding factor? (There’s a good chance they’ll say price. Push a little more. Ask leading questions about what a lower price may represent - fewer features, lack of integration, fewer seats etc. It’s rarely just about price.) What features or capabilities did the winning vendor offer that you do not?\nWhat were the overall perceived strengths and weaknesses of the vendor selected vs. the other evaluated? (Was it product-fit? Feature gap? Time lines? Services? etc.) Who was involved in the decision-making process? Were stakeholders opinions equal or weighted differently?\n\nTitle: A Framework for Creating the Right Win-Loss Analysis Questions\nURL: https://www.clozd.com/blog/a-framework-for-creating-the-right-win-loss-analysis-questions\nContent: ## Principle 1: Start with broad, open-ended questions to let the buyer tell their story.\n\nEvery buyer’s use case and evaluation experience is unique in some way. For that reason, you should start your buyer interview with broad, open-ended questions that encourage them to tell their unique story. This general context should then influence the nature and order of interview questions you ask during the rest of the conversation.\n\nConsider starting your win-loss interview with questions like:\n\n What prompted you to evaluate \\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_ solutions at this time?\n How did you first learn about \\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_?\n How did you structure and run your evaluation?\n What was your final decision?\n Why was that the right decision for your business? [...] ## Principle 2: Determine core topics and questions to cover in every interview.\n\nNext, you’ll want to leverage the momentum and learnings from the introductory questions to dive deeper. Determine several core topic areas that you wish to probe about (i.e., solution requirements, pricing, competitive landscape, sales experience, etc.). Develop a few open-ended questions for each topic area. Here are some common examples of topic areas and win-loss interview questions:\n\n### Product or Solution\n\n What specific requirements did the product/solution need to meet?\n Which capabilities were most important to you?\n What were your impressions of \\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_’s product/solution?\n What were its most significant strengths? Weaknesses?\n\n### Sales Experience [...] Throughout the interview, make sure that the majority of your questions are open-ended. Our favorite win-loss interview questions are what and why questions. They tend to introduce less bias and keep the interview conversational. Avoid asking quantitative, scale-based questions (i.e., On a scale of 1-5, how helpful was our sales team?). These questions are time-consuming because they require lengthy explanations to clarify the rating scale, and interviewees get confused easily. Quantitative questions also distract from the ultimate goal of hearing the buyer’s full story. Preserve as much time as possible for qualitative discovery. If there are quantitative questions that you need to ask, for trending or benchmarking purposes, ask them after the interview using a short survey.\n\nTitle: Win/Loss Analysis Templates - A Buyer's Guide | Growth Velocity\nURL: https://growthvelocity.com/win-loss-analysis-templates-a-buyers-guide/\nContent: A Framework for Creating the Right Win-Loss Analysis Questions by ClozdWe recommend A Framework for Creating the Right Win-Loss Analysis Questions by Clozd. The best attribute of this template is that it isn’t a template but a framework you can use to create the win/loss questions that work best for you. Sample questions illustrate five key principles, such as “start with open ended questions to let the buyer tell their story” and “determine core topics to cover in every interview” such as product, sales experience, and pricing.\n\nWin/Loss Topics by Ellen NaylorAlso recommended is Win/Loss Topics by Ellen Naylor. This is a one page summary of interview topics categorized by Relationship Health, Company Reputation, Service Issues, and Product Attributes. [...] Klue's Guide to Conducting Competitive Win-Loss InterviewsKlue’s Guide to Conducting Competitive Win-Loss Interviews suggests questions in 10 categories like Requirements, Competition, and Price.\n\n### (3) Templates for Analyzing Win/Loss Buyer Interviews\n\nA set of twenty buyer interviews will likely produce 200+ pages of interview transcripts.\n\nThis makes it critical to distill all that data down into the key patterns driving wins and losses.\n\nWe use a process of coding and counting to do this. The interview text is coded on dimensions like loss reason, decision criteria, and competitors. The prevalent patterns are then identified by counting: the total number of times, the number of times in wins, the number of times in losses and so on. [...] The Ideal Win/Loss Analysis Template by Satrix SolutionsWe recommend Satrix Solutions’s The Ideal Win/Loss Analysis Template. This template covers essential skills required of interviewers (strong listening skills, experience operating at senior levels, understands the competitive landscape). It makes recommendations about win/loss program criteria (which wins and losses will be targeted). It gives guidance about questions to ask (a carefully thought-out list of topics to steer the interview). Finally, it summarizes key strategies for the overall success of the program.\n\nTitle: Win Loss Analysis Template: Learn from Every Deal - Mural\nURL: https://www.mural.co/templates/win-loss-analysis\nContent: The win-loss analysis template includes:\n\n Deal notes: A section for capturing detailed information about the sales process and customer interactions.\n Summary: A space to synthesize the key findings from each win or loss.\n Key players: A section for identifying and documenting the roles of decision-makers and influencers.\n Timeline and milestones: A visual timeline to map out the entire sales journey, highlighting critical events and turning points.\n Elements of the deal: A breakdown of the deal into its component parts, such as pricing, product features, and legal.\n Insights and actions: A section for deriving actionable insights and outlining specific steps to improve future sales efforts. It should change over time, and likely even for each interview. Every sales opportunity is unique, so each conversation’s focus should consider that. That means an impartial 3rd party conducting the interviews, highlighting the key takeaways, and identifying the themes that emerge over time. {"parentUuid":"a6a7d2fa-c39b-4a53-a59f-bbc65ede6446","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"26330d16-85bd-46ca-a4d3-8f7566560d3c","agentId":"a11a14ba8af4b3589","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_013trmTEKWCMqUSJaPMQEdQM","type":"tool_result","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Detailed Results:\n\nTitle: Integrating Win-Loss Analysis Into Your Competitive Intelligence ...\nURL: https://www.evalueserve.com/blog/integrating-win-loss-analysis-into-your-competitive-intelligence-program/\nContent: ## Integrating Win Loss Analyses into Your Competitive Intelligence Program\n\nCompetitive Intelligence produces a broad swathe of information and data ranging from concrete figures (such as sales data) to more subjective measures (sentiment analysis of customer feedback, for instance). The win-loss ratio helps concretize and distill a lot of complex analysis into metrics that a child could comprehend.\n\nHowever, for that to work, and be maximally informative, you need to get data back from your marketing, sales, customer service, and support teams. Here’s the kind of information you can obtain to help inform a win-loss analysis: [...] Win-loss analysis allows a business to look both inwards and outwards with the same degree of scrutiny and obtain a properly objective understanding of the corporate position. It’s not merely about counting the number of deals won or lost, either. To get a fuller understanding of competitive performance, win-loss analysis must consider a range of variables including:\n\n Company size\n Brand identity\n Typical Client Persona\n Lead Source [...] ### What is Win-Loss Analysis?\n\nWithin CI, there’s a strand of analysis that focuses on deals won and lost. Win-Loss Analysis looks at a company’s performance in winning new business, securing clients, and building its customer base, breaking this down into two key metrics:\n\n Win-Loss Ratio – the proportion of deals won to deals lost.\n Win Rate – the proportion of all opportunities that a company wins.\n\nPerhaps more than any other metrics, these scores indicate how well a business is doing in its marketing and sales performance. They are concrete, difficult to argue with and, most importantly, these scores can be measured and improved.\n\nTitle: Win-Loss Analysis for Competitive Intelligence - Corporate Visions\nURL: https://corporatevisions.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-competitive-intelligence/\nContent: ### Penetrating the Shadow Layer\n\nWhen competitors win deals, they rarely reveal their true playbook. But continuous win-loss analysis can expose their tactics, as SPS Commerce discovered in a pivotal competitive deal.\n\n“We knew it was competitive,” explains Rob Fandrich, Director of Sales Engineering.\n\nAfter losing what seemed like a standard deal, their automated win-loss program kicked in, triggering a buyer feedback survey.\n\n“When our sales rep got to see the transcript and hear the voice recording, she went back into the battlecards for that competitor…Within a week after sharing new insight with the prospect, they formally signed with SPS Commerce.” [...] Your competitors are leaving breadcrumbs in every deal you win or lose.\n\nBut most companies don’t know where to look.\n\nSure, you can create competitor battlecards and feature comparison sheets. But those static tools don’t give you the precise insights you need to make a difference.\n\nThat’s why win-loss analysis is perhaps the most powerful (and underutilized) source of competitive intelligence.\n\nWin-loss analysis exposes not only what your competitors are doing, but how they’re positioning against you, where they’re finding success, and most importantly—where they’re falling short.\n\nSurface-level competitive intelligence is like reading tea leaves—it might make you feel informed, but you’re missing the real story. [...] ## What Your Competitors Don’t Want You to Know\n\nTraditional competitive intelligence focuses on what competitors want you to see: their features, pricing, and public positioning. It’s a comfortable illusion that keeps sales teams busy with battlecards and feature comparisons.\n\nBut our analysis of over 6,000 B2B deals reveals an uncomfortable truth: your competitors aren’t winning where you think they are.\n\nThe win-loss feedback from these deals exposes three clear patterns—patterns that reveal what competitors hope you never discover:\n\nMarket leaders who understand these patterns use them to predict and counter competitor moves before they happen.\n\nInstead of reacting to individual deal outcomes, they spot competitive trends and neutralize them early.\n\n### Turning Patterns into Action\n\nTitle: Accenture vs Deloitte: Who's winning in Trust and Innovation?\nURL: https://www.linkedin.com/posts/evan-escobedo_accenture-vs-deloitte-brand-equity-score-activity-7379963222751109120-rkVC\nContent: Excited to share our latest Head-to-Head Brand Equity Score™ Report. No fluff. No guesswork. Accenture vs. Deloitte. Two global consulting giants. But who’s winning in Trust and Innovation, specifically around AI? Some nuggets: => Accenture leads with big gains in Innovation (+18.8) and Reputation (+7.4) => Deloitte edges ahead in Trust (+1.3), signaling deeper familiarity. => Both face low awareness (34–39%), making earned + social narratives critical. Key Insight: Proof beats volume. Accenture’s AI leadership stories carry 41% higher impact—because outcomes matter more than noise. And the kicker? Both brands show goodwill gaps between public sentiment and media/social coverage. The winner will be whoever turns goodwill into belief next quarter. 👉 Swipe through the highlights below, [...] Excited to share our latest Head-to-Head Brand Equity Score™ Report. No fluff. The need for this approach is grounded in a measurement problem that most revenue organizations don’t realize they have. After analyzing 10,247 post-decision buyer conversations conducted on the User Intuition platform between January 2024 and December 2025, we found a 44-point gap between what buyers initially say drove their decision and what actually drove it. That gap — buyers citing price 62.3% of the time when price is the actual primary driver only 18.1% of the time — is invisible to CRM dropdowns, post-deal surveys, and even most human-moderated interviews that don’t probe deeply enough. Every finding is traced back to the actual buyer  ...    ## The Laddering Methodology: Why Depth Changes Everything The core technique that separates useful win-loss research from data collection exercises is laddering — a structured probing methodology that follows each buyer response through 5-7 successive levels of depth until the underlying decision logic becomes visible. Laddering originated in clinical psychology and was adapted for consumer research. In a win-loss context, it works like  ...    Level 1 — Surface answer: “We went with [competitor] because your pricing was too high.” Level 2 — Context probe: “When you say the pricing was too high, can you walk me through what that evaluation looked  ... This is the intelligence that explains why deals are won and lost, how buyers perceive you relative to competitors, what friction they encounter in the evaluation process, and what factors actually drive their final choice. VoB captures the decision, not just the experience. The distinction matters because VoC and VoB answer different questions, reach different populations, and drive different business outcomes. A strong VoC program with no VoB program produces a company that understands its customers  ...    Sales teams build playbooks based on what worked in won deals — which creates an optimization cycle around wins without understanding the much larger population of losses. The enablement materials, competitive responses, and process  ...    A Voice of Buyer program closes this blind spot by extending the feedback aperture to include the entire buyer population, not just the subset that purchased. For a deep dive into the methodology, see the complete win-loss analysis guide.  ...    An effective VoB program has five structural elements that distinguish it from ad hoc win-loss interviews or periodic competitive research projects. Continuous collection, not periodic projects. Buyer behavior and competitive dynamics shift continuously. A quarterly research project produces findings that are months old by the time they reach decision-makers. Continuous collection — interviewing buyers within 7-21 days of every purchase decision — ensures that intelligence is current. AI-moderated  ...    Full-decision coverage. Interview buyers from all three outcome categories: wins (to understand why you won and how to  ...    25-40% of qualified pipeline in many B2B markets. Structured probing methodology. The value of buyer conversations comes from depth, not breadth. Surface-level  ...    dropdowns. Structured laddering through 5-7 follow-up levels reveals the actual decision logic underneath the socially  ...    Cumulative intelligence architecture. Individual interviews produce anecdotes. A searchable, growing intelligence  ...    Traditional VoC programs organize around satisfaction metrics — NPS, CSAT, CES. A Voice of Buyer program organizes around decision intelligence — the structured understanding of how buyers evaluate, compare, and choose between alternatives. The Decision Intelligence Model captures four layers of buyer decision logic. Layer 1: Stated criteria. Voice of Customer (VoC) programs survey and interview your existing customers about product satisfaction, feature needs, and experience quality. Voice of Buyer (VoB) programs interview all buyers who evaluated your solution — winners, losers, and no-decisions — about how they made their purchase decision. VoC tells you what customers think about your product. VoB tells you why buyers choose or reject you. AI-moderated win-loss interviews address the core mid-market constraints directly. They eliminate the scheduling coordination problem that suppresses participation in human-moderated programs. They remove the interviewer bias that  ...    require an analyst to synthesize manually. The methodology matters here. A well-designed win-loss interview doesn’t ask buyers why they chose or rejected a vendor. It asks buyers to reconstruct their decision process — who was involved, what triggered the evaluation, what criteria emerged over time, what the internal debates looked like, and what the final decision felt like. Thursday, May 15, 2025\n\n##### The Anatomy of an Effective Win/Loss Program: Part Three ### Part 3: Choosing Internal vs. Third-Party Interviewers On your win / loss journey, once you know who you are going to talk to (prospect identification) and what you are going to talk to them about (interview guide design), the next decision is who will be talking to them. The most fundamental question is whether to use internal resources or to rely on third-party interviewers. Each option has benefits. To decide which is the best option, consider these elements: • Product expertise: The goal is to have the interviewee feel like they are having an intelligent conversation with someone who understands their business. No one will know your product more than internal resources, but highly trained third-party interviewers are often preferable as they are unbiased and therefore less likely to distort or influence  ...    • Interviewing expertise: Interviewing is an art form. With the ultimate goal of collecting the breadth and depth of information necessary to take action, deploying interviewers who are skilled in asking questions and encouraging  ...    interviewee to fill the space and provide more detail, and also making the interviewee comfortable enough so that they  ...    • Bandwidth: Particularly when thinking about a robust program consisting of dozens of interviews, bandwidth is an  ...    to schedule interviews is necessary to ensuring strong response rates. Writing up the transcript also takes time. Even if you are using AI to help with the transcription, the dialogue should still be reviewed and edited for clarity. This is where third parties can be a major value-add, as completing the interviews is their sole focus, as opposed to  ...    • Objectivity: Lastly, but perhaps the most important factor to consider, is objectivity. Anova’s research indicates that 60% of the time a salesperson does not get the full and accurate truth as to why they won or lost a deal if they debrief with a prospect themselves. This doesn’t mean a salesperson is doing anything nefarious. Prospects may be reluctant to give the person direct and honest feedback. Having someone internal who is removed from the situation (e.g., product manager / marketer) is a good way of increasing objectivity, and using a third party is the best way to obtain unbiased, independent feedback. Thursday, July 11, 2024 ##### Benefits of Win / Loss third party: How Anova’s Win / Loss program is different from typical home-grown solutions This blog was written by Harriet Peabody, Research Analyst at Anova. There are several advantages to using a third-party for your win/loss program compared to running it in-house: Increased Objectivity and Candid Feedback Reduced Bias: A key benefit is reduced bias. Customers may be hesitant to give completely honest feedback to your own sales team, fearing it might hurt their chances in future deals or damage relationships. A neutral third-party interviewer can create a safe space for customers to provide frank and open feedback. Using a third-party also allows for confidentiality, should the respondent wish for it, meaning you can still receive honest feedback from those who  ...    Unfiltered Insights: Third-party interviewers are unlikely to have preconceived notions or try to downplay negative  ...    Win / Loss Interviewing Skills: Third-party providers specialize in win / loss analysis and have trained interviewers experienced in conducting effective and insightful customer conversations. They know the right questions to ask and techniques to draw out valuable information. Anova’s interviewers are all experienced industry experts and are well versed in the Win / Loss space as well as the client’s area of business. This expertise shows through in the interviews and is clear to the contacts they are reaching out to as well, boosting Anova’s response rate, which is around 50-60%,  ...    Consultative approach: Anova has expertise in all aspects of program best practices, as we have been in the business for over 20 years. We will help you set up the logistics of the program, including the selection of deals to include in the program, tailoring the interview guide to your business’ needs, and staying in touch with updates and suggestions  ...    Anova has a broad client base and can provide competitive insights and benchmarking for numerous verticals, such as  ...    Reduced Time Commitment: Running a Win / Loss program internally requires time and resources from your sales team and other departments. A third-party handles the entire process, freeing up your team to focus on core business activities. With Anova, you will have a designated client team running your program and available for any day-to-day needs. Scalability: Third-party solutions can easily scale your win / loss program to accommodate a growing number of deals and interviews. This is particularly beneficial for companies with a high sales volume. In summary, not only is using a third-party easier and timesaving for you, but it boosts program success by getting to the root of any issue through thorough and objective feedback, promoting win / loss best practices, and offering competitive intelligence and industry benchmarking. Thursday, April 24, 2025\n\n##### The Anatomy of an Effective Win/Loss Program After over 20 years of working with companies to help them implement and build successful win / loss programs, we’ve heard every question under the sun. This series will explore the areas we often get asked about the most. As with many things in life, “it depends” is a common answer we resort to. Rarely is there a one size that fits all or off-the-shelf blueprint for win / loss program success. However, there are frameworks and things to keep in mind to  ...    organizations try to skip or treat as a check the box exercise. Even for the companies who really understand its importance, there is the question of how to effectively involve senior stakeholders with the program. This blog will  ...    A win / loss program is a great example of “you get what you put in”. Strong efforts will yield strong results. If It Sounds Too Good to Be True, It Probably Is. In general, teams are being asked to do more with less, both in terms of budget and FTEs. As such, the automation of processes is an important consideration in terms of cost and time savings. The rise of automation and generative AI has created exciting opportunities across industries, including win / loss. While the potential of AI is undeniable, it’s important to approach it with a realistic mindset. An advanced AI model,  ...    Practitioners of win / loss need to have a point of view and plan for taking advantage of automation and AI-driven opportunities, both for operational efficiency and insight generation purposes. Qual Matters – High-Quality, Qualitative Feedback from Qualified Respondents The best win / loss outcomes come from the programs with the highest-quality inputs and highest-quality deliverables. - Contact Us - High-quality inputs require buy-in from disparate stakeholders within an organization (especially sales) and a mechanism to source and vet in-scope win and loss opportunities with accurate decision-maker contact information in a reasonable post-decision timeframe. - Whether analyzed by human stakeholders or parsed by AI for buyer sentiment, the most actionable win / loss insights will be derived by the most detailed and nuanced data set. Live qualitative interviews, with their conversational flow and the opportunity to ask clarifying probes, remain the gold standard for generating this data. When the interview is conducted by an independent third party, it adds objectivity and removes potential bias. - Context matters – for example, if 42% of a company’s losses cite a perceived lack of value as a reason for not moving forward, is that better or worse than comparable firms? In addition to absolute trends, which can be tracked over time, it can also be helpful to understand how results compare to industry benchmarks. Implement a Win/Loss Program Launching a successful win/loss analysis program requires implementing seven key steps. You can create and deliver the program internally or hire a third-party research partner. In this example, the upcoming steps assume an internal design, launch and program administration 1. Identify a Program Coordinator The first step is to identify who will run the program. Often it will be someone in sales or marketing. This coordinator will be the go-to person, responsible for working directly with internal stakeholders and sales to gather information and finalize research instruments. The coordinator must be someone who can get things done and have enough authority to ensure that different areas of the company will work together. Because win/loss will not be successful without buy-in from sales, the coordinator must  ...    Once the coordinator has been selected, it’s time to set up a program kickoff meeting. It is critical that the head of  ...    stakeholders who will be involved or impacted by the win/loss program. Ask each stakeholder to share their objectives for the research and then develop a list of questions to use in the interviews. Also, determine the workflow for capturing information from recently closed won or lost deals, and agree on  ...    3. Create and Finalize the Interview Guide The best interview guides include input from key influencers within the organization and cover the most important stages of the sales cycle. The first step to explore is the vendor selection process. How did the prospect determine which vendors to evaluate, and what were their initial perceptions of the marketplace? Identify the attributes that were most important at the  ...    Get input from all areas of the company and be prepared to create several drafts before your questionnaire is fully  ...    4. Identify and Train Interviewers The next step is to select and train people who will conduct the research. Since it’s important to capture unbiased, honest feedback from prospects, the sales team should not be involved in conducting these interviews. Interviewers should be comfortable talking on the phone and able to lead informed, probing conversations. They should also have a  ...    6. in touch\n\n]\\(/contact)\n\n[Login](https://app.klue.com/)\n\n[GET A DEMO](#)\n\nArticle\n\n21 Win-Loss Analysis Statistics That Prove Buyer Feedback Is Your Biggest Advantage\n\nBy [Adam McQueen](/blog/authors/adam-mcqueen) 4 min read\nSeptember 4, 2025\n\nWe [surveyed 313 business leaders](https://klue.com/win-loss-trends-report) involved in win-loss programs to understand how they’re using buyer feedback to drive strategy – and where they’re seeing the biggest returns.\n\nFrom tighter messaging to faster sales cycles, here are 21 win-loss statistics that show how organizations are building smarter programs and seeing real business impact.\n\nWin-Loss Is a Strategic Investment\n\nInfographic of a 2025 win-loss statistic that shows the top strategic initiatives win-loss programs most often support within an organization.&#x20;\n\nThese stats show how companies are prioritizing win-loss and where budgets are going.\n\n1\\. 94% of respondents say their organization is maintaining or increasing its win-loss program budget.\n\n→ Only 5% reported a decrease, showing continued momentum even in tighter markets.\n\n2\\. 98% of programs now have some level of executive visibility.\n\n→ That includes 33% with full C-level visibility and 37% with significant visibility.\n\n3\\. Among enterprise organizations (1,001+ employees), 36% invest over $50K annually in win-loss.\n\n→ These companies are treating win-loss like a core business function, not an ad hoc initiative.\n\n4\\. 57% of enterprise companies have at least one full-time employee dedicated to win-loss.\n\n→ That number drops to 37% for small companies, showing a clear investment gap by size.\n\n5\\. 59% of enterprise companies use external research to support their win-loss efforts.\n\n→ External research can complement internal data, offering unbiased insights and professional interviewing.\n\nExecutive Impact & ROI\n\nAn infographic that shows a breakdown of the bigger impacts executives see from win-loss analysis. The infographic includes statistics showing what percentage of execs see improvements to messaging and positioning, win-rates, and sales cycles.&#x20;\n\nExecs aren’t just aware of win-loss—they’re using it to make high-level decisions.\n\n6\\. 70% of executives say they use win-loss insights to guide go-to-market messaging and positioning.\n\n→ This makes it the top strategic use case among surveyed execs.\n\n7\\. 56% of VP and C-level respondents say win-loss has helped increase win rates.\n\n→ A clear link between buyer feedback and bottom-line impact.\n\n8\\. 53% of executives report that win-loss insights have led to more effective sales cycles.\n\n→ Faster, more focused evaluations based on what real buyers care about.\n\nProgram Ownership and Structure\n\nAn infographic breaking down win-loss program ownership. What departments typically own win-loss, split up by percentage.&#x20;\n\nWho’s running win-loss—and how those programs are structured.\n\n9\\. Product Marketing owns the win-loss program at 41% of organizations.\n\n→ Followed by Sales (29%) and Marketing (16%).\n\n10\\. 70% of win-loss programs now support executive teams directly with buyer insight.\n\n→ They’re not just serving product and sales—they’re shaping leadership priorities too.\n\n11\\. Across all company sizes, 40% of deals are analyzed on average through win-loss.\n\n→ That’s a significant volume of buyer feedback fueling decisions.\n\n12\\. 70% of programs analyze deals within one month of close.\n\n→ But only 3% collect feedback within 24 hours—showing there’s room to move faster.\n\nInsight Distribution and Usage\n\nWin-loss only works if the insights get shared and acted on.\n\n13\\. 44% of teams share win-loss insights quarterly, making it the most common cadence.\n\n→ Monthly (16%) and ad-hoc (27%) are next most common.\n\n14\\. Among companies sharing insights monthly or weekly, 66% use executive summaries.\n\n→ Slack/Teams (62%) and sales enablement materials (47%) are also popular methods.\n\n15\\. 91% of programs use win-loss to support sales, making it the most commonly supported team.\n\n→ Followed by product (77%), execs (70%), product marketing (69%), and marketing (67%).\n\nWin-Loss + Competitive Intelligence\n\nMany orgs are merging win-loss and CI—and seeing stronger outcomes because of it.\n\n16\\. 67% of win-loss programs are managed alongside competitive intelligence.\n\n→ Another 10% plan to integrate in the future.\n\n17\\. Teams with fully integrated win-loss and CI programs are twice as likely to report “transformational impact.”\n\n→ 12% of integrated programs reported transformational business impact, versus just 6% of partially integrated and 2% of non-integrated programs.\n\n18\\. 64% of respondents say integrating CI and win-loss leads to better competitive positioning.\n\n→ Other benefits: deeper insights (56%), better enablement (52%), and stronger product strategy (29%).\n\nThe Rise of AI in Win-Loss\n\n\n\nAI is reshaping how teams run analysis and extract insights from interviews.\n\n19\\. 41% of companies not currently using AI in their program say they plan to.\n\n→ Only 19% say they have no plans to adopt AI.\n\n20\\. On average, 21% of win-loss work is now AI-augmented.\n\n→ Including transcription, insight tagging, trend detection, and more.\n\n21\\. Top AI applications include transcription (63%), insight extraction (50%), and trend analysis (39%).\n\n→ Other use cases include sentiment analysis (32%) and automated reporting (33%).\n\nFinal Takeaway\n\nThese 21 stats tell a clear story: buyer feedback is no longer a side project, it’s a strategic advantage.\n\nThe most mature programs are investing in headcount, external research, and AI tools. They support every team from sales to product to the C-suite. And they’re not just listening to buyers – they’re acting on what they learn.\n\nSHARE THIS POST\n\n[](https://www.linkedin.com/sharing/share-offsite/?url=https://klue.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-statistics)[](https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https://klue.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-statistics)[](https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?text=Hello%20world\\&url=https://klue.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-statistics)\n\nFillable Template\n\nWin-Loss Analysis\n\nTemplate for Your Executive Summary\n\nDeliver a 10/10 win-loss summary to your exec team with this free fillable template\n\n[DOWNLOAD NOW](https://go.klue.com/win-loss-analysis-template)\n\nBrowse by category\n\n[AI](https://klue.com/blog/category/ai)\n\n[Battlecards](https://klue.com/blog/category/battlecards)\n\n[Competitive Enablement](https://klue.com/blog/category/competitive-enablement)\n\n[Win Loss](https://klue.com/blog/category/win-loss)\n\n[Sales](https://klue.com/blog/category/sales)\n\n[Product Marketing](https://klue.com/blog/category/product-marketing)\n\n[Klue](https://klue.com/blog/category/product-updates)\n\nBrowse by type\n\n[ARTICLES](https://klue.com/content-type/article)\n\n[COURSES](https://klue.com/content-type/course)\n\n[Product](https://klue.com/content-type/product)\n\n[Guides](https://klue.com/content-type/guide)\n\n[reports](https://klue.com/content-type/report)\n\n[templates](https://klue.com/content-type/template)\n\nWant to see Klue in action?\n\nLet’s do it. Compete Community\n\nLearn and connect with your peers\n\n]\\(https://community.thecompetenetwork.com/)\\[\n\nCustomer Stories\n\nExplore how our customers win with Klue\n\n]\\(/customers)\\[\n\nSubscribe to our Newsletter\n\nCompetitive news and strategies — straight to your inbox\n\n]\\(/coffee-compete)\\[\n\nJob Board\n\nThe best PMM & competitive jobs in one spot\n\n]\\(https://competitive-enablement-jobs.klue.com/)\n\n\\[\n\n\n\nHow to Plug Competitive Intel Into Your Agentic Workflows\n\nJoin us for a live “show-and-tell” session where we wire CI data into AI workflows in real-time.\n\n]\\(https://klue.registration.goldcast.io/webinar/132cc59d-262f-40bc-a56d-30cdafd69350)\n\n\\[\n\n\n\n\n\nCoffee & Compete Toolkit\n\n]\\(https://go.klue.com/guide-to-competitive-battlecards-101)\\[\n\n\n\n\n\nHow to Build a Win-Loss Program\n\n]\\(/blog/how-to-start-a-win-loss-program)\\[\n\n\n\n\n\nCompetitive Enablement Maturity Model\n\n]\\(/maturity-model)\\[\n\n\n\n\n\nCompetitive Battlecard Course\n\n]\\(/battlecards-course)\n\n[Company](#)\n\n\\[\n\n\n\nAbout\n\nLearn about our team, our mission and our vision for how organizations will compete in the future\n\n]\\(/about)\n\n\\[\n\nCareers\n\nJoin our team\n\n]\\(/careers)\\[\n\nNews\n\nWhat we’re up to\n\n]\\(/news)\\[\n\nContact\n\nHow to get in touch\n\n]\\(/contact)\n\n[Login](https://app.klue.com/)\n\n[GET A DEMO](#)\n\nArticle\n\nThe 31 Best Win-Loss Questions You Should Be Asking\n\nBy [Adam McQueen](/blog/authors/adam-mcqueen) 6 min read\nSeptember 4, 2025\n\nWhat are the best win-loss analysis questions to ask during an interview with a buyer after a deal?\n\nAn honest starting point for anyone looking to cut their teeth on [win-loss analysis](https://klue.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-guide).\n\nAt the same time, this is the wrong way to go about it. \n\nBefore we put on our detective hats, dust off our line of questioning, and channel our inner Sherlock Holmes… stop!\n\nTake a breath. And read on.\n\nIn this blog, we’ll cover:\n\nThe questions you should be asking internally before you start conducting win-loss interviews or [win-loss surveys](https://klue.com/blog/win-loss-surveys)\n\nThe categories of win-loss questions you should be asking your buyer after a deal\n\nThe questions to ask within each category\n\nHow to evolve your questions over time so that you can deliver insights that are most relevant to the business decisions your executive team is making today.\n\n[New call-to-action](https://cta-redirect.hubspot.com/cta/redirect/2241989/f1b13c3c-c64a-4c40-88d9-0c960754151d)\n\nThe Win-Loss analysis questions you need to ask internally\n\nThe first win-loss questions you should be asking happen well before the buyer interview even begins.\n\nYour initial questions need to be geared to your stakeholders, which when it comes to win-loss analysis, are the departmental leaders within your organization. \n\nThese are the people who will make business decisions based off of your win-loss findings, meaning that your work should be tailored to what they’re looking to learn. \n\nThis alignment is critical: our 2025 Win-Loss Trends Report found that [98% of win-loss programs now have executive visibility](https://klue.com/win-loss-trends-report), and [76% of execs surveyed say](https://klue.com/win-loss-trends-report) they rely on win-loss data to sharpen competitive strategy. Starting with clear stakeholder objectives ensures your program delivers insights that truly drive business priorities.\n\nThis is called identifying your learning objectives.\n\n“When launching your win-loss program, you need to know what the individual objectives are for each one of your functional areas, so I always recommend going to the leader within each group first,” said Ryan Sorley, CEO and Founder of DoubleCheck Research, [at the Winner’s Circle event hosted by the Compete Network](https://youtu.be/N9RQUKb1tdc).\n\nWhat are the internal questions you should ask, then?\n\nIt starts with two simple questions:\n\nWhat is most important to you right now?\n\nWhat are you looking to learn from win-loss analysis?\n\nThese are broad questions, obviously. But it sets the stage for you to dig deeper, by asking:\n\nIf you had this information today, what would you do with it?\n\nGrab your shovel and keep digging. This is where you get a more coloured picture of how win-loss can bring value to different departments, and ensure that the insights you bring don’t just sit in a slide deck collecting dust.\n\nAre you just going to keep it on your laptop and look at it? \n\nWill this bake into your product roadmap? \n\nIs this useful for sales enablement sessions you’re planning next quarter?\n\nDoes your next board meeting need better [competitive intelligence](https://klue.com/blog/competitive-intelligence) brought to the table?\n\nConducting internal discovery not only makes you more confident entering a win-loss interview that you’re not tossing darts at a board blindfolded, but it also unlocks the secret to success for your win-loss program.\n\nInternal buy-in.\n\n“Those leaders start to lean into the program that much more. They get excited about the outcome that is yet to come, and they start to visualize what it is that you’ll be producing for them,” says Ryan Sorley.\n\n“You end up getting a lot of people to have a sense of ownership of the win-loss program. It becomes stickier and the program takes off as a result of it.”\n\nKeep a core list of Win-Loss analysis questions, but be agile\n\nThink about your company’s messaging a year ago.\n\nI’m sure it’s drastically different than today. More empathetic, considering the times that we’re currently in, perhaps?\n\nThis is just one example of how constantly you — and your competitor’s — go-to-market, product offerings and messaging are changing.\n\nAs such, you need to be asking questions that generate feedback relevant to these business decisions and strategies so that your executives can make more informed decisions, faster than the rest of the market.\n\nWhile certain win-loss questions are evergreen, the ability to ask ones that unveil buyer intelligence on how your strategies are landing in the market today is what transforms a good win-loss program, into a great one [explained Valerie Bonaldo, Director of Product Marketing Competitive Intelligence at Seismic, at the Winner’s Circle.](https://youtu.be/N9RQUKb1tdc)\n\n“When doing win-loss, we have a core set of questions that we stick with because we want to know the importance of what the buyer is looking for and how our sales team is engaging with them across the sales cycle.\n\nBut maybe we would want to measure how a recent product launch went? How some new messaging was presented in the sales cycle? We’ll pull out data specific to that within the interview guide and look for the qualitative results around what the customer’s real impressions were and how something resonated.”\n\nIt’s in those moments where win-loss can work its magic, especially when executives are dying to know why a big strategic bet they’ve made is (or isn’t) bringing desired results.\n\n“The great thing about being able to do a quarter-over-quarter win-loss program is to be able to go back and look to see what was consistent in our surveying and in our questions, as well as where we see the conversations shift as we brought forth new initiatives and new features.”\n\nAsk Win-Loss analysis questions covering the entire buyer journey\n\nNo matter what, make sure you’re asking questions that acknowledge every step in the buying process. \n\nGenerally, there are 10 steps in the buyer journey you need to consider [when conducting a win-loss analysis interview](https://klue.com/blog/how-to-nail-win-loss-interviews).\n\nbest b2b win-loss analysis questions\n\nHere are a few questions that you can ask within each category:\n\nThe Role\n\nHow did you play a role in the evaluation process?\n\nWhat are your responsibilities above and beyond evaluations like these?\n\nTell me about your previous experience with vendors like us. \n\nAwareness\n\nWhat made you realize you needed a vendor partner in this space?\n\nHow many voices at your company were asking for a change that led you to evaluate your options?\n\nWhat were the main issues with your previous vendor partner (if applicable)\n\nEvaluation Bias\n\nWhat did you think of our company before the evaluation started?\n\nWhat did you think of \\[insert competitor] before the evaluation started?\n\nHow have your thoughts about our company, our competitors and the space in general changed over time?\n\nRequirements\n\nHow did you go about requirements gathering?\n\nHow well did \\[company] align with your requirements?\n\nWhat were your must-haves?\n\nConsideration\n\nWho else did you evaluate in your decision? \n\nExplain the factors that helped narrow down your decision-making.\n\nWhat were the strengths and weaknesses you saw across all vendors?\n\nBuying committee\n\nWho was responsible for what in the buying process?\n\nWho were the most influential people in the decision-making process?\n\nHow do you think the buying process would have been different had there been fewer voices at the table?\n\nProduct Demo\n\nWhat was the most memorable part of the demo experience?\n\nHow did our demo compare with some of the other finalists?\n\nWhat would you have like to see more of?\n\nProducts and Services\n\nHow would you describe our company’s product strengths and weaknesses?\n\nHow much was customer service emphasized during the evaluation process?\n\nWhat would make our product better?\n\nPrice\n\nHow did our pricing model compare to our competitors?\n\nHow important was price to you and your team in the first place?\n\nTo what extent did you feel like our pricing matched the value we offered?\n\nSales Process\n\nWhat was missing from the sales process and what did you like about it?\n\nHow did our sales process differ from others?\n\nHow can we make our sales process more effective?\n\nThat’s only 30 win-loss analysis questions…\n\nGuilty! There is indeed a 31st question. It’s one that comes at the end of your interview — and happens to be the most fun of the bunch. \n\nIf you want to check that question out, as well as the secret to nailing your win-loss interview courtesy of the experts over at DoubleCheck Research… [it’s all here waiting for you.](https://go.klue.com/guide-for-conducting-competitive-win-loss-interviews)\n\n[New call-to-action](https://cta-redirect.hubspot.com/cta/redirect/2241989/d8912830-bd5a-483c-92d4-851bfbfd5244)\n\nSHARE THIS POST\n\n[](https://www.linkedin.com/sharing/share-offsite/?url=https://klue.com/blog/31-best-win-loss-questions)[](https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https://klue.com/blog/31-best-win-loss-questions)[](https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?text=Hello%20world\\&url=https://klue.com/blog/31-best-win-loss-questions)\n\nEXPERT GUIDE\n\nThe Ultimate Guide to Win-Loss Interviews\n\nGet 50+ interview questions for your next win-loss call.\n\n[DOWNLOAD NOW](https://go.klue.com/guide-for-conducting-competitive-win-loss-interviews)\n\nBrowse by category\n\n[AI](https://klue.com/blog/category/ai)\n\n[Battlecards](https://klue.com/blog/category/battlecards)\n\n[Competitive Enablement](https://klue.com/blog/category/competitive-enablement)\n\n[Win Loss](https://klue.com/blog/category/win-loss)\n\n[Sales](https://klue.com/blog/category/sales)\n\n[Product Marketing](https://klue.com/blog/category/product-marketing)\n\n[Klue](https://klue.com/blog/category/product-updates)\n\nBrowse by type\n\n[ARTICLES](https://klue.com/content-type/article)\n\n[COURSES](https://klue.com/content-type/course)\n\n[Product](https://klue.com/content-type/product)\n\n[Guides](https://klue.com/content-type/guide)\n\n[reports](https://klue.com/content-type/report)\n\n[templates](https://klue.com/content-type/template)\n\nWant to see Klue in action?\n\nLet’s do it.
